It’s a very beautiful thing when the wound becomes the doorway.” </p>\n <cite>— Henry Shukman</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Henry Shukman</strong> (<a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/mountaincloudzencenter/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">@mountaincloudzencenter</a>) teaches mindfulness and awakening practices to a wide range of students from all traditions and walks of life. Henry is an appointed teacher in the Sanbo Zen lineage and is the Guiding Teacher of <a href=\"https://www.mountaincloud.org/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Mountain Cloud Zen Center</a>. He has an MA from Cambridge and an MLitt from St Andrews and has written several award-winning books of poetry and fiction.</p> \n<p>Henry’s essays have been published in <em>The New York Times</em>, <em>Outside</em>, and <em>Tricycle</em>, and his poems have been published in <em>The New Republic</em>, <em>The Guardian</em>, <em>The Sunday Times</em> (UK), and <em>London Review of Books</em>. He has taught meditation at Google, Harvard Business School, UBS, Esalen Institute, Colorado College, United World College, and many other venues. He has written of his own journey in his memoir <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/One-Blade-Grass-Finding-Memoir/dp/1640092625\" target=\"_blank\"><em>One Blade of Grass: Finding the Old Road of the Heart, a Zen Memoir</em></a>.</p> \n<p>Henry has also recently created a new meditation program, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"http://originallove.org\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>Original Love</strong></a>, which aims to provide a broad, inclusive path of growth through meditation.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em><a to Understanding the True Nature of the Universe by Robert Lanza and Bob Berman | Amazon</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"http://www.holotropic.com/holotropic-breathwork/about-holotropic-breathwork/\" noopener\">Vipassana Meditation | Dhamma.org</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://tim.blog/2021/08/18/random-show-kevin-rose-nft-crypto/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">The Random Show — href=\"https://meowwolf.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Immersive Art Experiences | Meow Wolf</a></li>\n</ul> \n<h3>SHOW NOTES</h3> \n<ul>\n <li>Why did Henry once identify with Typhon, the mythological beast who lived under the volcano Etna, and how did this lead to his first foray into meditation? [09:51]</li>\n <li>What was Henry’s introduction into the world of meditation? [15:19]</li>\n <li>What does Henry mean by the term “awakening?” [16:40]</li>\n <li>Is awakening a repeatable phenomenon, or is it something that only needs to occur once? Henry conveys how Zen master Yamada Koun described it, and shares his own first experience. [20:02]</li>\n <li>Was Henry under the influence of any substances when this happened? In retrospect, what does he think brought it on? [32:12]</li>\n <li>Was Henry later able to replicate this state of awakening at will, or control it in any way? [54:16]</li>\n <li>How long was Henry’s first Zazen meditation, and why does he think it moved him so powerfully? [1:01:25]</li>\n <li>What is a koan? It’s a very beautiful thing when the wound becomes the doorway. I think there’s always that potential with a wound, but so much of the time we tend to accrete protection over a wound and sort of stay away from a wound and avoid it and live as if it weren’t there.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“There’s another dimension, another aspect, another face of our experience of this very moment in which we are totally part of everything.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“In awakening, one thing vanishes, that sense of self, and another thing appears, which is what that sense of self was occluding.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“All space had disappeared. There was no distance anywhere. I felt like my nose was pressing against the end of time. My nose was touching the furthest reaches of the universe because it was all just here. It was one reality without space, without time.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“I knew that I had found the answer, but I hadn’t even been asking a question.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“I felt that I had found sort of the answer to life, which I hadn’t been looking for and wasn’t interested in. But now it was like I knew I could die and my life had been fulfilled.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>[On his first experience with Zazen meditation] “It’s not like I had some marvelous enlightenment experience right then, but I did have a sense in that first sit that this was a way of contacting life.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“The first thing to say about koans is they are not riddles to be solved, and our mind wants to make them that.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“I suppose I still am a bit of a troubled soul, but I’m a happy one.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“It’s not like it’s ‘Awakening or bust.’ That’s a wrong mindset.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“In any level of any zone of practice, love is usually the thing, somehow or other, that triggers the transformation that opens up things.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p><strong>“It’s like love to find that we’re part of everything. It’s like love to find that this nothing—no thing—is producing everything, generating everything, infinite generosity. That’s like love.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Henry Shukman</br></p> \n<p>\n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  <strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" Ferriss","enclosureType":"audio/mpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://rss.art19.com/episodes/c62bd9af-f4f6-4bb9-8022-754a79ef46df.mp3","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i0.wp.com/tim.blog/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/Henry-Shukman.jpeg?resize=600%2C480&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn13@2020_6/2021/09/27/16-55-13-817_284327b0bac47a2f.webp","https://i1.wp.com/tim.blog/wp-content/themes/timferriss/inc/blocks/podcast-link/images/apple-podcast.png?w=600&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn34@2020_2/2021/09/27/16-55-39-683_48857a198d68c0f0.webp","https://tim.blog/wp-content/themes/timferriss/inc/blocks/podcast-link/images/spotify.svg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn29@2020_1/2021/09/27/16-55-40-035_1e63253b1371dcc9.svg","https://tim.blog/wp-content/themes/timferriss/inc/blocks/podcast-link/images/overcast.svg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn92@2020_2/2021/09/27/16-55-39-774_32d4d52d4ec2eced.svg","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/timferriss?i=9HdyCPiUo9U:olkAljLyePg:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn11@2020_2/2021/09/27/16-55-39-711_ab5829538a33af9f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/timferriss?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn4@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-39-667_59f7ed02281d28ab.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/timferriss?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn55@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-39-626_07e7fa252280afa4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/timferriss?i=9HdyCPiUo9U:olkAljLyePg:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn76@2020_5/2021/09/27/16-55-39-731_0b76407940d452c3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/timferriss?i=9HdyCPiUo9U:olkAljLyePg:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn72@2020_6/2021/09/27/16-55-39-768_daf2e0d0711d5ca4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/timferriss/~4/9HdyCPiUo9U":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn21@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-39-674_ff2f70484c03e6bb.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1632761713088},{"createdTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:13","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:13","title":"Machiavelli for Women: 7 Power Strategies for the Workplace","link":"https://tim.blog/?p=57215","description":"\n<p>The following is an excerpt from the new book,&#160;<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Machiavelli-Women-Playbook-Getting-Ahead/dp/1982121750\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong><em>Machiavelli for Women: Defend Your Worth, Grow Your Ambition, and Win the Workplace</em></strong></a>. The author,&#160;<strong>Stacey Vanek Smith</strong>&#160;(<a href=\"https://twitter.com/svaneksmith\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">@svaneksmith</a>), is a longtime public radio reporter and host. She currently hosts NPR’s&#160;<em>The Indicator from Planet Money</em>, a daily podcast covering business and economics. She has also served as a correspondent and host for NPR’s&#160;<em>Planet Money</em>&#160;and&#160;<em>Marketplace</em>. Stacey is a native of Idaho and a graduate of Princeton University, where she earned a BA in comparative literature and creative writing. She also holds an MS in journalism from Columbia University.<br><br><strong>Enter Stacey…</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Niccolò Machiavelli. Today, he is probably best known as a ruthless power monger, devoid of ethics and compassion. The phrase most often associated with him, “The ends justify the means” (which Machiavelli never actually wrote but probably would have heartily agreed with), has turned him into an apologist for sociopaths, tyrants, and megalomaniacs the world over.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>I think this is a gross misunderstanding of both the man and his work.&#160;<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Prince-Niccol%C3%B2-Machiavelli/dp/B08WZHBPW6\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Prince</em></a><em>&#160;</em>does not condone random cruelty or tyranny or violence. It is a remarkably sober look at how people take power and how they can best hold on to it and grow it. Machiavelli was an incredibly clear-eyed original thinker who might just be history’s first true champion of real talk. For that reason, there could be no better guide for the workplace.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In the five hundred years since Machiavelli wrote&#160;<em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Prince-Niccol%C3%B2-Machiavelli/dp/B08WZHBPW6\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">The Prince</a></em>, a lot of things have changed: We have electricity, the combustion engine, computers, and antibiotics. We’ve even split the atom. People, though, haven’t changed one bit. And for that reason, Machiavelli’s advice about navigating the workplace has proven to be quite timeless.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Machiavelli’s strategies are powerful tools in the modern workplace, especially for women, people of color, and other often-marginalized workers. Here are 7 of my favorite Machiavellian power tips.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>#1 — Always Get the Truth, Even If It Hurts&#160;</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Part of seeing a situation clearly is having people you trust who can offer their outside observations. They can help you see what you don’t want to (or can’t) see. This means helping you assess different situations and colleagues and also giving you feedback. Machiavelli was big on feedback. “A Prince,” he writes, “ought always to take counsel” (Chapter XXIII). Machiavelli saw honest feedback as the primary way a prince could protect himself against flatterers and yes-men. (Machiavelli was death on flatterers and yes-men) “There is no other way of guarding oneself from flatterers except letting men understand that to tell you the truth does not offend you” (Chapter XXIII). Feedback feels like risky exposure, but Machiavelli saw it as powerful protection for a prince and a way to get necessary information. The ability to hear the truth—or the “truth” of people’s impressions and opinions—will make you stronger and smarter and help you succeed. Machiavelli was careful to say that you should not open yourself up in this way to just anybody. Ask the people who are key to enabling you to move up within a company; ask people you respect; ask the people you trust.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>#2 — Cultivate Your Network&#160;&#160;</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Having people you trust and people who can help you in the workplace is crucial (in life, too!). Building a network of people at work or in your profession is absolutely essential to building a career. You should have a mix of people: people who are high up in the company, people who are colleagues, and those who are more junior—also people at other companies who can make you aware of opportunities that come up outside of your bubble. Machiavelli preached the importance of a network hard. The smart prince, he writes, “is defended by being well armed and having good allies” (Chapter XIX). Having a strong network is essential to rising in any profession. Wall Street CEO Sallie Krawcheck says she always remembers advice she got from her friend Carla Harris (a senior banker at Morgan Stanley): “All the important decisions about your career are made when you’re not in the room. People decide to hire you, fire you, promote you, fund you, send you on the overseas assignment, all when you’re not there. So how do you ensure that you have someone in the room fighting for you? I would strongly argue that you need to have in place your Personal Board of Directors. Those are your mentors, your sponsors, your confidantes.” </p>\n\n\n\n<span id=\"more-57215\"></span>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>#3 — Stand Up for the Less Powerful&#160;</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>This might sound like the antithesis of Machiavelli. After all, shouldn’t you suck up to powerful people and mercilessly crush those who have less power? I mean, NO OF COURSE YOU SHOULD NOT DO THAT! But, also, Machiavelli advised against it.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>One of Machiavelli’s main pieces of advice in a situation where you don’t have a lot of power is to stick up for other people who also don’t have much power. “The prince,” he writes, “ought to make himself the head and defender of his less powerful neighbors, and to weaken the more powerful amongst them.” The reason? Not only are you weakening the powers that be and creating a potential opening for yourself, but also the people you speak up for will be loyal to you and will fight for you and your ideas in the future. As Machiavelli puts it, “By arming them, those arms become yours” (Chapter XX).&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In fact, Machiavelli advises against speaking up on behalf of people who have&#160;<em>more&#160;</em>power than you. Don’t jump in if the boss gets interrupted by the intern in a meeting. The benefits of smacking down the intern are minimal, and you risk being seen as a suck-up and a bully. Also, your soul would probably die a little, and that’s never good.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>#4 — If It Comes Down to Being Liked or Respected… Choose Respect</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>This is a situation that happens to women at work a lot. Often, women in the workplace end up in a double bind: caught between qualities people associate with a “good woman” (being modest, compassionate, putting others first, soft-spoken, nurturing) and the qualities people associate with a good leader (being independent, firm, outspoken, assertive, not caring too much what people think).&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Machiavelli addresses this particular bind many times in&#160;<em>The Prince</em>. As it turns out, the prince is in a similar situation: it’s crucial that he be loved by his people, but the prince also needs people to fear his wrath, follow his laws, and, of course, pay his taxes. “Here comes in the question whether it is better to be loved rather than feared, or feared rather than loved. . . . We should wish to be both; but . . . if we must choose between them, it is far safer to be feared than loved” (Chapter XVII).&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Still, this approach comes with real consequences, especially for women. Where harsh or domineering men might be respected or tolerated in leadership positions, people often react very differently to an assertive or domineering woman. But sometimes getting the job done correctly involves being blunt, direct, and assertive. It might not be easy, and you will likely get pushback, but do it.<br><br><strong>#5 — Watch Your Back</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>I can’t talk about Machiavelli without addressing some of the advice he gives about guarding yourself against nemeses. Machiavelli wrote a LOT about nemeses in&#160;<em>The Prince</em>. He thought the ability to spot troublemakers and toxic people was crucial for a prince: “The ruler is not truly wise who cannot discern evils before they develop themselves, and this is a faculty given to few” (Chapter XIII). Truly, it is not always easy to spot a troublemaker or a toxic person. But Machiavelli does point out a couple of things to watch for.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Beware the flatterer: Machiavelli saw flatterers as very dangerous to a prince. He even has an entire chapter in&#160;<em>The Prince&#160;</em>titled, “How Flatterers Should Be Avoided” (Chapter XXIII). This is not to say that if somebody compliments your work, you should respond with grave suspicion. But flattery can often be used to manipulate people, so make sure that’s not happening to you.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Beware the Debbie Downer: If someone you work with is extremely negative, it’s probably a good idea to keep your distance and to be careful what you say to them. “As soon as you have opened your mind to a malcontent,” Machiavelli warns, “you have given him the material with which . . . he can look for every advantage” (Chapter XIX).&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>#6 — Avoiding Risk Is Risky — When in Doubt, Take Action</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Asking for a raise or a promotion or applying for a new job or launching a new project requires enormous risk and vulnerability. Wanting to avoid that risk and the possibility of rejection is a big reason people (especially, statistically, women) shy away from negotiating or asking for more. But avoiding risk and waiting for “the perfect moment” is not a safe choice.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Machiavelli’s advice: when in doubt, take action. This advice probably came from his own frustration with the Florentine council’s constant waffling (they were his bosses). They never wanted to choose sides in the countless skirmishes and battles going on around them. It was, in fact, partially due to that very waffling that Florence lost its republican government and ended up back in the hands of a despot.<br><br>“I know that many say a policy of neutrality is the safest option,” he wrote in a letter to a friend. “I believe to the contrary that neutrality is an exceedingly dangerous path.” And for a prince, Machiavelli declares that waffling will lead “in most instances to their destruction” (Chapter XXI).&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>#7 —&#160; Embrace the Struggles that Arise; They Are Setting You Up for Success&#160;</strong><br><br>The professional world can be incredibly difficult and unfair, especially for women and other marginalized workers. It can involve an incredible amount of perseverance and struggle. That’s not okay or fair, but it does offer its own kind of gift. Machiavelli observed that princes who had to struggle for their kingdoms actually did better in the long run than the princes who had everything handed to them. “They who . . . acquire with difficulty . . . keep with ease” (Chapter VI).&#160;<br><br>That might sound trite or saccharine, but Machiavelli was neither of those things, and he felt so strongly about the value of overcoming difficulties in helping to shape a prince, he has a rare Zen moment in&#160;<em>The Prince&#160;</em>of the “Things happen&#160;<em>for&#160;</em>me not&#160;<em>to&#160;</em>me” variety. “Fortune,” he writes, “especially when she desires to make a new prince great . . . causes enemies to arise and form designs against him, in order that he may have the opportunity of overcoming them. . . . Princes become great by vanquishing difficulties and opposition” (Chapter XX).</p>\n\n\n\n<p>###</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Excerpted with permission from&#160;<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Machiavelli-Women-Playbook-Getting-Ahead/dp/1982121750\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong><em>Machiavelli for Women: Defend Your Worth, Grow Your Ambition, and Win the Workplace</em></strong></a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/timferriss?a=3QRl0jWi9dc:fP1iJ_wPxJA:V_sGLiPBpWU\"><img <figcaption>\n  Illustration via \n  <a href=\"http://99designs.com/tim\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">99designs</a>\n </figcaption>\n</figure> \n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\">\n <p>“When we’re safe and secure and we feel we matter to others and that they have our back, our potential and our resources come out.”</p>\n <cite>— Dr. Sue Johnson</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Dr. Sue Johnson</strong> (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/Dr_SueJohnson\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">@Dr_SueJohnson</a>) is a leading innovator in the fields of couple therapy and adult attachment. She is the primary developer of Emotionally Focused Couples and Family Therapy (<a href=\"https://iceeft.com/what-is-eft/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">EFT</a>), which has demonstrated its effectiveness in over 30 years of peer-reviewed clinical research. Sue has received numerous awards in recognition of her development of EFT, including the American Psychological Association’s “Family Psychologist of the Year” and the Order of Canada.</p> \n<p>Her best-selling book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Hold-Me-Tight-Conversations-Lifetime/dp/031611300X\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Hold Me Tight</em></a> — with more than one million copies sold—has taught countless couples how to enhance and repair their love relationships. The book has since been developed into a relationship enhancement program called <a href=\"http://holdmetightonline.com\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Hold Me Tight Online</a>. Her most recent book for clinicians, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Attachment-Theory-Practice-Emotionally-Individuals/dp/146253824X\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Attachment Theory in Practice</em></a>, delineates the promise of attachment science for understanding and repairing relationships.</p> \n<p>As the founding director of the International Centre for Excellence in Emotionally Focused Therapy (<a href=\"http://iceeft.com\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">ICEEFT</a>), Sue trains counselors in EFT worldwide and provides guidance to 80 affiliated centers. She consults for the US and Canadian militaries and is a popular presenter and speaker for the general public.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" [06:25]</li>\n <li>EFT has a high success rate in studies with distressed couples. How is success clinically defined for these couples? [09:33]</li>\n <li>What scales are used to assess marital satisfaction and bond, and how has this played out in research? [13:33]</li>\n <li>What is a hold me tight conversation? [19:41]</li>\n <li>Examples of how hold me tight conversations can take shape. [21:01]</li>\n <li>How a hold me tight conversation might work for someone who has a tendency to isolate (or feels isolated). [32:01]</li>\n <li>On just how common such isolation is, and how “dependency” has become such a dirty word. [40:17]</li>\n <li>Attachment parenting vs. sleep training. [44:36]</li>\n <li>What are micro-interventions from Rogerian models of therapy (aka evocative questions)? [54:18]</li>\n <li>How does Sue respond to a client who can’t really answer how they’re feeling in their body? [1:02:47]</li>\n <li>How would someone “up the ante” in the context of a hold me tight conversation, and why does it usually have the opposite of its intended effect? [1:09:41]</li>\n <li>How does Sue help someone work through anger? [1:11:35]</li>\n <li>Why Sue is fascinated by Winston Churchill, and reading she recommends for someone who wants to know more about him. [1:15:02]</li>\n <li>Tango talk. Since it famously takes two to tango, what kind of arguments do tango couples get into? [1:20:33]</li>\n <li>How would Sue advise a couple who’s very much in love but their sexual spark has faded? [1:33:44]</li>\n <li>Advice for a couple in which the woman has a more sustained sex drive than the man. [1:43:11]</li>\n <li>What brought about the development of Sue’s Hold Me Tight Online program, and what can people expect from it? [1:48:44]</li>\n <li>Parting thoughts. [1:53:17]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3 id=\"more-dr-sue-johnson-quotes\">MORE DR. SUE JOHNSON QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p>“Love is an ancient, wired-in survival code.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“If you can’t find comfort in the arms of another to heal from trauma, it’s bad news.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“What attachment science tells us is that emotional isolation is toxic for human beings. We found out that in the pandemic, but we still don’t get it.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Once you feel seen, accepted, and held, there’s a natural human growth process that happens.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“The way human beings have survived through the centuries is through tuning in to others, reading their cues, collaborating, cooperating, moving close, supporting—that’s the way we’ve survived. And if you look at the problems facing our world right now, we’d better be learning from this science or we’re not going to survive. We’ve got to be able to come together.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Shutting down and numbing out is a fragile strategy you can’t keep up for your whole life. It shatters under any kind of pressure.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Parenting is a moving target. You accommodate your child, then your child changes.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“We understand that to be emotionally alone traumatizes a child. We need to apply that to adults because in that sense, we never grow up. Attachment goes from the cradle to the grave.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Some of the cliches we have about love are really awful misinformation. But one of the cliches that’s really true—and this is true in most religions—is that when we’re loved, we grow and expand.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Attachment gives us a map to how we dance together with the people we love and where those dances go in terms of outcome. It gives us a map to our own vulnerabilities and emotions. It tells us how supremely sensitive we are to signals of rejection or abandonment by other people, and that this sensitivity is wired in.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“We all know on some deep, visceral level how much we need others, and the strongest among us can accept that and learn how to connect.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Tango is about attunement, and so is love.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“In the end, what none of us can bear is the feeling that we’re alone and that we don’t matter to another human being.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“Passion is about feeling safe enough to be completely absorbed in the experience and let it take you over. Passion is about full engagement.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<p>“It’s very hard to be open and physiologically responsive when you’re afraid and guarding yourself all the time.” — Dr. Sue Johnson</p> \n<h3 id=\"people-mentioned\">PEOPLE MENTIONED</h3> \n<ul>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://twitter.com/jimcoan?lang=en\" target=\"_blank\">James Coan</a></li>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.verywellmind.com/john-bowlby-biography-1907-1990-2795514\" target=\"_blank\">John Bowlby</a></li>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Rogers\" target=\"_blank\">Carl Rogers</a></li>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/literature/1953/churchill/biographical/\" target=\"_blank\">Winston Churchill</a></li>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://twitter.com/Oprah?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Eauthor\" target=\"_blank\">Oprah Winfrey</a></li>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.bdperry.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Bruce D. nothing wrong in always being dissatisfied; always look for improvement.”</p>\n <cite>— Sir James Dyson</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Sir James Dyson</strong> is the&nbsp;founder and chairman of&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.dyson.com/en\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Dyson</a>. Through investment in science and technology and working alongside&nbsp;Dyson’s 6,000 engineers and scientists, he develops products that solve problems ignored by others. Sir James was elected a&nbsp;<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_2015\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Fellow of the Royal Society</a> in 2015 and appointed to the&nbsp;<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_Merit\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Order of Merit</a> in the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_New_Year_Honours\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">2016 New Year Honours</a>.&nbsp;He&nbsp;was awarded a CBE in 1996 and a&nbsp;<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knight_Bachelor\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Knight Bachelor</a>&nbsp;in&nbsp;2007.</p> \n<p>James is the founder of <a href=\"https://www.jamesdysonfoundation.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">James Dyson Foundation</a>, inspiring the next generation of engineers&nbsp;through scholarships, engineering workshops, university partnerships, and the annual James Dyson Award, an international student design competition. In 2017 James established <a href=\"https://www.dysoninstitute.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">The Dyson Institute of Engineering and Technology</a>, where undergraduate engineers&nbsp;pay zero tuition and&nbsp;earn&nbsp;a&nbsp;full&nbsp;salary while completing their degree studies and working on real-life projects&nbsp;alongside world-experts in&nbsp;Dyson’s&nbsp;global engineering, research,&nbsp;and technology&nbsp;teams.</p> \n<p>James is the author of the new book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Invention-Life-James-Dyson/dp/1982188421\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong><em>Invention: A Life</em></strong></a>, the story of how he came to be an inventor himself and built Dyson, leading it to become one of the most inventive technology companies in the world.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em><a rel=\"noreferrer [05:39]</li>\n <li>What did James study at university? [08:15]</li>\n <li>What does it mean to think like an engineer? [09:43]</li>\n <li>Were enthusiasm, fascination, and curiosity qualities that were fostered by anyone in James’ orbit as a youth, or did they come gradually through experiences along the way? [10:52]</li>\n <li>How might James suggest parents encourage these qualities in their children? [12:06]</li>\n <li>What’s the origin story of the original Dyson vacuum? [14:05]</li>\n <li>How many tries did it take to get a prototype that worked as intended, and what did the process of financing and development look like? [19:26]</li>\n <li>Why invention is not about being brilliant — it’s about being logical and persistent. [23:07]</li>\n <li>What compelled James to persist for as long as it took to create the first ideal prototype? How long would it have taken for him to throw his hands up and admit defeat if that prototype had never materialized? [25:53]</li>\n <li>Was developing the first prototype James’ full-time occupation at the time? Did he have a plan B if things didn’t work out? [29:29]</li>\n <li>Once the desired prototype appeared, why was the vacuum industry initially resistant to its innovations? [30:17]</li>\n <li>When did James finally get a foothold on the commercialization of his now-famous vacuum? [34:41]</li>\n <li>Encouraging feedback reinforcing James’ belief in this new product must have been overwhelming by this point, right? [36:46]</li>\n <li>How did James’ wife handle the uncertainty of prototype development and commercialization? [39:17]</li>\n <li>Even though surveys suggested that customers wouldn’t want to see the dirt and dust sucked up by the Dyson vacuum, James decided to make the cleaner’s bins see-through anyway. Why? [40:15]</li>\n <li>How expensive were the first Dyson vacuums compared to their contemporary competition? How hard was it to get retailers to carry them, and why did the customer demographic run counter to expectations for a “premium” brand? [42:16]</li>\n <li>Rather than designing to a price, what does James aim to achieve with his inventions? [44:42]</li>\n <li>Who is Akio Morita, and what does James appreciate about him? [46:35]</li>\n <li>Other inventors, designers, or engineers who stood out for James when he was developing his chops. [47:56]</li>\n <li>What led to the development of the Dyson Airblade? [51:38]</li>\n <li>On the Airblade being the first product Dyson targeted for an industrial rather than consumer audience, why this made James uncomfortable, and what its major selling points were. [54:39]</li>\n <li>Why, in spite of being fantastically efficient, James has to consider the Dyson washing machine a favorite failure. [57:36]</li>\n <li>Why the Dyson N526 electric car was canceled, and features James hopes to see carried over into future projects. [1:00:36]</li>\n <li>Dyson is a privately held company. What makes James uncomfortable about going public or accepting investment funding? [1:09:00]</li>\n <li>What prompted James to write&nbsp;<em>Invention: A Life</em>&nbsp;and start the Dyson Institute of Engineering and Technology? [1:16:46]</li>\n <li>On experience as baggage that can get in the way, applying cross-disciplinary curiosity to solve problems in unique ways, and the power of asking naive questions. [1:24:27]</li>\n <li>What would James’ billboard say? [1:28:44]</li>\n <li>Parting thoughts. [1:29:46]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3>MORE GUEST QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong><em>“Experience is a baggage that can get in the way.”</em></strong><br>—&nbsp;<em>James Dyson</em></br></p> \n<p><strong><em>“Drop your fear of failure; don’t be afraid of failure.”</em></strong><br>—&nbsp;<em>James Dyson</em></br></p> \n<p><strong><em>“Invention is not about being brilliant; it’s about being logical and persistent.”</em></strong><br>—&nbsp;<em>James Dyson</em></br></p> \n<p><strong><em>“I did want to call my book </em>A Life of Failure<em> because failure’s exciting and you learn from failure. If you’re taught something and then what you do works, you haven’t really learned anything. You haven’t learned what doesn’t work, which is usually more interesting.”</em></strong><br>—&nbsp;<em>James Dyson</em></br></p> \n<p><strong><em>“The best questions are naive questions.”</em></strong><br>—&nbsp;<em>James Dyson</em></br></p> \n<p><strong><em>“Whenever I look at anything, I wonder how it works, and then I wonder how it could work better. Could I make it work better? Is there a technology I could use? Is there a way I can reconfigure it? Is there a radical breakthrough I could do for lateral thinking that would make a huge difference?”</em></strong><br>—&nbsp;<em>James Dyson</em></br></p> \n<p>\n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  <strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://podcasts.apple.com/us/podcast/530-sir-james-dyson-founder-of-dyson-and-master/id863897795?i=1000534124244\" target=\"_blank\">Apple Podcasts</a>, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/episode/3nxFsj5DwFx1qlFhIbZkoh?si=Oqp2Y29NSNyigb-eVR6qsw&amp;dl_branch=1\" target=\"_blank\">Spotify</a>, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://overcast.fm/+KebupqmsE\" target=\"_blank\">Overcast</a>, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.stitcher.com/podcast/the-tim-ferriss-show\" target=\"_blank\">Stitcher</a>, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://castbox.fm/channel/id1059468?country=us\" target=\"_blank\">Castbox</a>, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://podcasts.google.com/?feed=aHR0cHM6Ly9yc3MuYXJ0MTkuY29tL3RpbS1mZXJyaXNzLXNob3c&amp;ved=0CAAQ4aUDahcKEwjI_ezXzuDnAhUAAAAAHQAAAAAQAQ\" target=\"_blank\">Google Podcasts</a>, or on your favorite podcast platform.</em></strong></em></strong> \n </meta></p> \n<h3>PEOPLE MENTIONED</h3> \n<ul>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeremy_Fry\" target=\"_blank\">Jeremy Fry</a></li>\n <li><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.bfi.org/about-fuller\" target=\"_blank\">R. [1:25:55]</li>\n <li>What is BlockFi, and what are its rewards and risks compared to traditional banking alternatives? Does Kevin see future governmental regulation putting a damper on the potential of this kind of platform, or will it bolster its use by the average consumer? [1:30:13]</li>\n <li>Only in the world of DeFi: how self-repaying loans work through the Alchemix protocol. [1:34:06]</li>\n <li>While Kevin welcomes sensible blockchain regulation in the US to clarify the intangibles for the average consumer, what are the potential pitfalls of crypto that worry him? [1:36:26]</li>\n <li>Dollar cost averaging and risk management to avoid the dreaded panic sell. [1:39:13]</li>\n <li>Why I don’t make an investment I can’t hold for at least three years. human knowledge.”</p>\n <cite>— Jimmy Wales</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p>Internet and technology entrepreneur <strong>Jimmy Wales</strong> (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/jimmy_wales\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">@jimmy_wales</a>) is founder of the online nonprofit encyclopedia <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Main_Page\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Wikipedia</a> and cofounder of the privately owned Wikia, Inc., including its entertainment media brand <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fandom_(website)\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Fandom</a>, powered by Wikia. Jimmy serves on the board of trustees of the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikimedia_Foundation\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Wikimedia Foundation</a>, the nonprofit charitable organization he established to operate Wikipedia.</p> \n<p>In 2019, Jimmy launched <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://wt.social/\" target=\"_blank\">WT Social</a>—a news-focused social network. In 2006, Jimmy was named to <em>Time</em> magazine’s list of the 100 most influential people in the world for his role in creating Wikipedia.</p> \n<p>In 2021, inspired by his family quiz nights during COVID-19 lockdown, Jimmy created <strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"http://www.quiznightbeyond.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Quiz Night Beyond</a></strong>—a website where people can create and play quizzes online with family and friends wherever they may be.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" [10:41]</li>\n <li>Why Jimmy would label himself more as a non-believer than an atheist, and how he came to a point of religious disbelief after being raised Methodist and carrying out a flawed experiment involving Santa Claus and G.I. Joe. [12:13]</li>\n <li>What did Jimmy aspire to be when he was growing up? [17:08]</li>\n <li>What was it that compelled Jimmy to study finance and markets? [18:37]</li>\n <li>Were there any investors who Jimmy looked up to at this time? What influenced the way he built his portfolio in these early years?</li>\n <li>What wisdom about building a portfolio does Jimmy wish he understood when he was younger, and what investor could have imparted such wisdom? [20:01]</li>\n <li>When and how did Jimmy’s interest in Objectivism enter into the picture, what does he see as the most common myths and misconceptions about its creator, Ayn Rand? [22:18]</li>\n <li>Whether or not you agree with the points Ayn Rand (or anyone) is trying to make, Jimmy considers critically thinking them through — rather than just picking a side based on a knee-jerk impression — to be the important thing. [24:46]</li>\n <li>How Usenet was hijacked by its own design, why Twitter succumbs to many of the same problems, and how Jimmy hoped Wikipedia could be designed as a vehicle for healthier human discourse. [26:42]</li>\n <li>Recommended reading. [32:30]</li>\n <li>How did Jimmy go from finance and markets to founding companies? [36:14]</li>\n <li>When was Jimmy able to leave his finance job behind and dive fully into entrepreneurial endeavors? What made this possible? [42:04]</li>\n <li>How was Jimmy’s company discovered by a major television network, and how was their symbiotic deal established? [47:24]</li>\n <li>With a lot of great ideas but only a limited number of resources, how did Jimmy choose what shots to take when setting out in the world of tech startups during the dotcom boom? [50:35]</li>\n <li>When the deal fell through, was Jimmy’s company able to use the momentum it had built, or did it have to start from scratch in altering course? What sacrifices had to be made in order to weather the storm, and how did these hardships affect the direction taken by the nascent Wikipedia? [54:06]</li>\n <li>Why did Wikipedia thrive while Nupedia became an internet footnote? [1:00:30]</li>\n <li>How did Jimmy first come across the wiki software that would provide the building blocks for Wikipedia? [1:05:22]</li>\n <li>What were some of the early decisions that made Wikipedia catch in its first few months? [1:07:37]</li>\n <li>Were there any significant mistakes made in the early days of Wikipedia that either ended up needing to be fixed or that, like a vestigial tail, have remained in the system? [1:11:42]</li>\n <li>The Steak Knives Analogy: why it’s better to design for the benefit of nice people rather than for the deterrence of bad people. [1:16:57]</li>\n <li>What motivates great Wikipedia contributors, and how are they kept happy? [1:20:25]</li>\n <li>What organizational decisions aim to cultivate a positive contributor culture? [1:22:19]</li>\n <li>Has Jimmy always been a self-described “pathological optimist,” or was it something he had to work at? [1:00:00]</li>\n <li>Does Jimmy have any practices to help him stay the course of optimism if he loses his way? [1:28:23]</li>\n <li>Aside from Wikipedia, what other projects occupy Jimmy’s time at present? What’s the inspiration and purpose behind his new Quiz Night Beyond website?[1:30:25]</li>\n <li>What would Jimmy’s billboard say? [1:40:35]</li>\n <li>Parting thoughts. [1:42:06]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3>MORE JIMMY WALES QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p>“Imagine a world in which every single person on the planet is given free access to the sum of all human knowledge.” \n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  — Jimmy Wales\n </meta></p> \n<p>“You wouldn’t know it from watching TV debates or going on Twitter, but most people are pretty reasonable and will say, ‘Look, I disagree with this, but it should be presented in a fair way that the defenders of it would identify.'” \n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  — Jimmy Wales\n </meta></p> \n<p>“I’m a pathological optimist, so I always think everything’s going to be great. That’s not correct, by the way; everything is not always going to be great. But that’s the way I feel and approach things.” \n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  — Jimmy Wales\n </meta></p> \n<p>“If you design for the worst people, then you’re failing design for good people.” \n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  — Jimmy Wales\n </meta></p> \n<p>“A great Wikipedian, in my view, is someone who really takes seriously the values and ideas of Wikipedia, like neutrality for example, quality for example, reliable sources. And they take all those things as more important than any particular, say, political opinion they might have.” \n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  — Jimmy Wales\n </meta></p> \n<p>“We’ve got a lot of problems on this planet. We need as many smart, educated people to figure them out as we possibly can.” \n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  — Jimmy Wales\n </meta></p> \n<p>“Try not to be a troll on the internet.” — Jimmy And that’s not the case.” </p>\n <cite>— Paul Conti, MD</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Paul Conti, MD</strong> is a graduate of Stanford University School of Medicine. He completed his psychiatry training at Stanford and at Harvard, where he was appointed chief resident and then served on the medical faculty before moving to Portland and founding a clinic.</p> \n<p>Dr. Conti specializes in complex assessment and problem-solving, as well as both health and performance optimization, serving patients and clients throughout the United States and internationally, including the executive leadership of large corporations. His new book is <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Trauma-Invisible-Epidemic-Works-Heal/dp/1683647351\" target=\"_blank\"><strong><em>Trauma, the Invisible Epidemic: How Bessel van der Kolk | Amazon</a></li>\n</ul> \n<h3>SHOW NOTES</h3> \n<ul>\n <li>How did Paul end up in psychiatry? [06:29]</li>\n <li>Who wrote the foreword to <em>Trauma: The Invisible Epidemic</em>? [10:18]</li>\n <li>What is Paul’s personal history with trauma? [11:07]</li>\n <li>How does Paul suggest people think about and define the word “trauma?” How do certain subsets of trauma differ from one another? [16:54]</li>\n <li>How might someone take the first step in dealing with trauma that places itself in the forefront and makes it difficult to focus on anything else? [19:20]</li>\n <li>What does Paul think of the current state of trauma treatment? In what ways does it fail, and how can it be improved? [26:16]</li>\n <li>If we’re not doing a good job in the US, where <em>can</em> the most effective trauma treatment be found? [28:39]</li>\n <li>What does it take to really unearth the causes (and ideal treatment) of trauma beyond applying the imperfect trauma symptom inventory? [30:08]</li>\n <li>The good, the bad, and the ugly within the skill set of treating trauma. [32:45]</li>\n <li>“We shouldn’t ask why the addiction, we should ask why the pain.” -Dr. Gabor Maté [38:36]</li>\n <li>Modalities, frameworks, and tools that Paul has found to be particularly helpful in working with individuals who have trauma in their background. [41:29]</li>\n <li>Antidotes for hypervigilance. [46:34]</li>\n <li>What most helped Paul come to terms with his brother’s death by suicide, and how did it set the course for him to start medical school at age 25? [54:42]</li>\n <li>What is the basic structure of <em>Trauma: The Invisible Epidemic</em>? [1:00:09]</li>\n <li>Paul shares his favorite story from the book. [1:04:00]</li>\n <li>What is selective abstraction? [1:07:01]</li>\n <li>How might trauma promote learned helplessness that can push someone toward further trauma and even death? [1:09:47]</li>\n <li>What does Paul think about the use (and potential for abuse) of psychedelics in treating trauma? [1:12:39]</li>\n <li>With a number of arrows in the quiver, as Paul says, how does he prioritize options when treating someone for trauma? Might empathogens be included among these options? [1:19:12]</li>\n <li>An instance when Paul might prescribe an anti-psychotic (and what he finds objectionable about this name). [1:26:31]</li>\n <li>How can chlorpromazine (Thorazine) be helpful in these cases? [1:29:34]</li>\n <li>The most helpful way to approach lithium as a treatment. [1:33:11]</li>\n <li>What about trazodone? Does it carry any addictive or psychological risks? [1:37:21]</li>\n <li>Dopamine as a currency of distress. [1:40:09]</li>\n <li>What is a diathesis–stress model? [1:40:47]</li>\n <li>Credible resources recommended for listeners interested in learning more about trauma. [1:43:09]</li>\n <li>Parting thoughts. [1:45:25]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3>MORE PAUL CONTI QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong>“Dopamine is like a currency. People say, ‘What does dopamine do?’ It’s like saying, ‘What does a dollar do?’ It depends on where we’re spending it.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“We can feel helpless and the world can seem hopeless. And that’s not the case.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If you’re not getting help the first, second, third time, keep trying. There’s help there to be had.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“Vicarious trauma comes from, really, this wonderful fact that we can be empathic and empathically attuned to other people and we can feel what they’re feeling. That’s a wonderful thing that we can do that for one another, but it also makes us so susceptible to other people’s suffering and pain.”</strong><br>\n  <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n   —&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti\n  </meta></br></p> \n<p><strong>“We’ve evolved a system that purveys mental healthcare largely without attention to the actual human being. And this is a huge problem.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If we just take symptoms, we will get it wrong.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“A person should be able to talk about what’s going on inside of them because it’s burying those unhealthy seeds, so to speak, that then compounds original trauma into something that can end up being far, far worse with a whole cascade of problems.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“There’s so much that gets compounded when the original trauma gets pushed outside of consciousness and outside of communication.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“More pain and suffering means people are more likely to repeat maladaptive patterns that lead them to more trauma.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If trauma arouses shame, then the trauma itself immediately alters our perception mechanisms that we can use to understand the trauma and navigate our way through it.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Paul Conti</br></p> \n<p>\n <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n  <strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" noopener\">99designs</a>\n </figcaption>\n</figure> \n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\">\n <p>“The first negotiation is really a negotiation with yourself to move from being focused on what I’m right about and you’re wrong about to getting curious about why we see this so differently.” </p>\n <cite>— Sheila Heen</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Sheila Heen</strong>&nbsp;is a&nbsp;<em>New York Times</em>&nbsp;best-selling author, founder of&nbsp;<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"http://triadconsultinggroup.com\" target=\"_blank\">Triad Consulting Group</a>, and a deputy director of the&nbsp;<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.pon.harvard.edu/category/research_projects/harvard-negotiation-project/\" target=\"_blank\">Harvard Negotiation Project</a>&nbsp;at Harvard Law School, where she has been a member of the faculty for 25 years. Sheila specializes in particularly difficult negotiations, where emotions run high and relationships become strained. She often works with executive teams, helping them to resolve conflict, repair professional relationships, and make sound decisions together. In the public sector, she has provided training for the New England Organ Bank, the Singapore Supreme Court, the Obama White House, and theologians struggling with disagreement over the nature of truth and God. <br><br>Sheila is co-author of the&nbsp;<em>New York Times</em>&nbsp;bestsellers&nbsp;<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Difficult-Conversations-Discuss-What-Matters/dp/0143118447\" target=\"_blank\"><strong><em>Difficult Conversations: How to Discuss What Matters Most</em></strong></a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Thanks-Feedback-Science-Receiving-Well/dp/0143127136\" target=\"_blank\"><strong><em>Thanks for the Feedback: The Science and Art of Receiving Feedback Well (even when it’s off base, unfair, poorly delivered, and, frankly, you’re not in the mood)</em></strong></a>. She has written for the&nbsp;<em>Harvard Business Review</em>&nbsp;and the&nbsp;<em>New York Times</em>—as a guest expert and contributor to the “Modern Love” column—and she has appeared on NPR, Fox News, CNBC’s&nbsp;<em>Power Lunch</em>, and shows as diverse as&nbsp;<em>Oprah</em>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<em>The G. Gordon Liddy Show</em>. She has spoken at the Global Leadership Summit, the Nordic Business Forum, the Smithsonian, Apple, Google, and Microsoft. Sheila is a graduate of Occidental College and Harvard Law School. She is schooled in negotiation daily by her three children.</br></br></p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" [11:28]</li>\n <li>What are the three categories of difficult conversations, and what is the underlying structure they have in common? [16:13]</li>\n <li>How understanding the underlying structure of these three conversations helps you find your way if one doesn’t go as planned. [25:36]</li>\n <li>How can we convey genuine curiosity in these conversations without unintentionally coming off as condescending? What if this isn’t the first time this conversation has happened and it’s getting heated? [28:25]</li>\n <li>Sometimes the person with whom you’re conversing is just seeing things from a vastly different perspective. Sheila shares a personal story. [38:13]</li>\n <li>When a disconnect like this seems evident, how can each party come to a better understanding of where the other is coming from? [41:45]</li>\n <li>What is a statement against interest, and how can it be used? [46:28]</li>\n <li>A good apology versus a bad apology: what’s the difference? [48:37]</li>\n <li>Rewriting the scripts for bad apologies — particularly when each party has very different ideas about conflict resolution and resilience. [58:00]</li>\n <li>What does it mean to “give voice to the parts?” [1:15:39]</li>\n <li>What are first, second, and third position skills, and what does it mean to step into the third position? [1:16:51]</li>\n <li>The importance of setting expectations about — and learning over time — how the people in your life behave (this includes you). [1:21:22]</li>\n <li>What would Sheila’s best self say to her business partner and co-author in the midst of collaborating on a book’s third edition after a flare-up? [1:22:35]</li>\n <li>In personal and professional relationships, we often have feedback for the other about how we think they need to change. But what if we present obstacles as shared problems? [1:28:09]</li>\n <li>Blame-absorbers versus blame-shifters. [1:30:05]</li>\n <li>Heroes, villains, and victims. [1:34:21]</li>\n <li>Parting thoughts. [1:43:08]</li>\n <li>Additional post-game thoughts from Sheila about the question that plays like background music to many difficult conversations, how to avoid making every difficult conversation a spring cleaning when your relationship simply needs some light spot-dusting, and a 60-day experiment. [1:46:27]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3>MORE SHEILA HEEN QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong>“Some people have trouble with some conversations, and the same conversation is easy for someone else. And it’s partly because of whatever the story is that you tell about yourself and who you are trying to be.”</strong>&nbsp;<br>—&nbsp;Sheila Heen<br><br><strong>“The first negotiation is really a negotiation with yourself to move from being focused on what I’m right about and you’re wrong about to getting curious about why we see this so differently.”</strong>&nbsp;<br>—&nbsp;Sheila Heen<br><br><strong>“Part of what I’m listening for in a difficult conversation is I’m just trying to understand what do you see and how do you interpret it? We each have implicit rules about how the world is supposed to work. Do we have different implicit rules here? Then it helps me to put those puzzle pieces on the table and try to fit them together, understanding that some of them just won’t fit. And that’s okay.”</strong>&nbsp;<br>—&nbsp;Sheila Heen<br><br><strong>“It’s not just that we have difficult conversations in our most important relationships; those conversations&nbsp;are&nbsp;the relationship. That if we find a way to have them productively or constructively, then the relationship will thrive, whether it’s a personal or professional relationship, and/or is where we start to disengage because you just don’t get it and you won’t listen, and the relationship starts to fray.”</strong>&nbsp;<br>—&nbsp;Sheila Heen<br><br><strong>“‘What am I missing?” is actually a great question, partly because they’re going to be so thrilled to be able to tell you. They’re going to take that invitation, but genuinely they can see things that you can’t.”</strong> <br>\n              <meta charset=\"utf-8\">\n               —&nbsp;Sheila Heen\n
